î€€e Synthesis Lectures on Digital Circuits and Systems series is comprised of 50- to 100-page books
targeted for audience members with a wide-ranging background. î€€e Lectures include topics that
are of interest to students, professionals, and researchers in the area of design and analysis of digital
circuits and systems. Each Lecture is self-contained and focuses on the background information
required to understand the subject matter and practical case studies that illustrate applications. î€€e
format of a Lecture is structured such that each will be devoted to a speciï¬c topic in digital circuits
and systems rather than a larger overview of several topics such as that found in a comprehensive
handbook. î€€e Lectures cover both well-established areas as well as newly developed or emerging
material in digital circuits and systems design and analysis.

ABSTRACT
î€€is book, Ampliï¬ers: Analysis and Design, is the second of four books of a larger work, Funda-
mentals of Electronics. It is comprised of four chapters that describe the fundamentals of ampliï¬er
performance. Beginning with a review of two-port analysis, the ï¬rst chapter introduces the mod-
eling of the response of transistors to AC signals. Basic one-transistor ampliï¬ers are extensively
discussed. î€€e next chapter expands the discussion to multiple transistor ampliï¬ers. î€€e coverage
of simple ampliï¬ers is concluded with a chapter that examines power ampliï¬ers. î€€is discussion
deï¬nes the limits of small-signal analysis and explores the realm where these simplifying assump-
tions are no longer valid and distortion becomes present. î€€e ï¬nal chapter concludes the book
with the ï¬rst of two chapters in Fundamental of Electronics on the signiï¬cant topic of feedback
ampliï¬ers.
Fundamentals of Electronics has been designed primarily for use in an upper division course
in electronics for electrical engineering students. Typically such a course spans a full academic
years consisting of two semesters or three quarters. As such, Ampliï¬ers: Analysis and Design, and
two other books, Electronic Devices and Circuit Applications, and Active Filters and Ampliï¬er Fre-
quency Response, form an appropriate body of material for such a course. Secondary applications
include the use with Electronic Devices and Circuit Applications in a one-semester electronics course
for engineers or as a reference for practicing engineers.
KEYWORDS
active loads, ampliï¬er conï¬gurations, ampliï¬ers, cascaded ampliï¬ers, cascode, cur-
rent mirror, current sources, Darlington ampliï¬ers, distortion, feedback ampliï¬ers,
feedback topologies: shunt-series feedback, series-series feedback, series-shunt feed-
back, shunt-shunt feedback; gain, multistage ampliï¬ers, optimal biasing, power am-
pliï¬ers: class A, class B, class AB, push-pull; return diï¬€erence, transistor modeling,
two-port networks: h-parameters, g-parameters, z-paramters, y-parameters; thermal
modeling

Preface
î€€e basic principles of the operation of the four basic active elements, as described in Book 1,
provide a good foundation for further study of electronic circuitry. Analog ampliï¬ers constitute
a major class of electronic circuitry and are a primary component in many applications. While
it has been shown that electronic devices are basically non-linear, ampliï¬ers typically operate
within a region of incremental linearity. î€€at is, a region where small variation in the input pro-
duces linearly ampliï¬ed variation in the output. Analysis of such systems uses the principle of
superposition: DC (or bias) conditions are separated from the AC (or variational) components
of the input and output of an ampliï¬er. î€€e term â€small-signal analysisâ€ refers the use of linear
models. â€Large-signal analysisâ€ implies operation near the transition between operational regions
of an active device: such large-signal operation is typically non-linear and leads to distorted am-
pliï¬cation.
î€€is book begins with a review of two-port analysis. î€€is review provides a basis for model-
ing transistors: they are most commonly modeled as two-port networks for small-signal analysis.
At low frequencies the BJT is modeled by an h-parameter two-port and the FET as a modi-
ï¬ed hybrid- two-port. Simple ampliï¬ers are approached by observing the previously observed
region of operation that appeared between the two logic states of an inverter. All single-transistor
ampliï¬er conï¬gurations are analyzed and performance characteristics compared.
Multiple transistor ampliï¬er circuitry is initially approached through cascading single tran-
sistor ampliï¬ers using capacitive coupling. Only after the basic concepts are mastered, are the more
complex circuits studied. Compound transistor conï¬gurations, such as the Darlington circuit, and
direct-coupled ampliï¬er stages are studied. As with single-transistor ampliï¬ers, the previously
observed linear region in ECL logic circuits leads to the study of emitter-coupled and source-
coupled ampliï¬ers. Common integrated circuit practices such as current source biasing and active
loads are discussed.
Power ampliï¬ers provide a good counterexample to the use of small-signal analysis. By
necessity, the output of a power ampliï¬er is not small and consequently may contain distorted
components. Both harmonic and intermodulation distortion analysis techniques are introduced
and compared. Ampliï¬er conversion eï¬ƒciency is discussed for class A, B, and AB power ampli-
ï¬ers. î€€ermal considerations are presented using simple heat transfer models and are related to
power ampliï¬er design criteria and limitations.
Feedback principles are introduced initially as a technique to stabilize ampliï¬er gain, re-
duce distortion, and control impedance. î€€e various conï¬gurations are introduced and analyzed

xvi
PREFACE
through two-port network analysis techniques. Improvement in frequency response is mentioned
as an additional beneï¬t but discussion is left for the next book in this series.

C H A P T E R
5
Single Transistor Ampliï¬ers
Circuits containing single Bipolar Junction and Field Eï¬€ect Transistors are capable of providing
ampliï¬cation in six basic conï¬gurations: three conï¬gurations for each type of transistor. In the
common-emitter and common-collector conï¬gurations for BJTs, the input signal is injected at
the base of the transistor: in the common-base conï¬guration it is injected at the emitter. Similarly,
FET conï¬gurations have the input injected in the gate for common-source and common-drain
conï¬gurations, and at the source for the common-gate conï¬guration. î€€ese six single-transistor
conï¬gurations are basic building blocks of ampliï¬er design.
î€€e focus of attention in this chapter is on the signiï¬cant performance characteristics of
each ampliï¬er conï¬guration: the voltage gain, current gain, input resistance and output resistance.
Each conï¬guration has a unique set of performance characteristics that are dependent on the
transistor characteristics as well as the conï¬guration and value of surrounding circuit elements.
î€€is chapter primarily investigates discrete ampliï¬ers where biasing is accomplished with voltage
sources and resistances: later chapters will highlight BJT biasing with current sources as developed
in Section 3.6 (Book 1). î€€e performance of transistor ampliï¬ers with current source biasing can
be directly derived from the results presented in this chapter. î€€e frequency range of interest is the
socalled midband region: that range of frequencies below where the transistor characteristics begin
to change with increasing frequency and above where any capacitive elements (many circuits have
no capacitors and therefore the midband frequency range begins at DC) have signiï¬cant eï¬€ect
on the circuit performance
Although ampliï¬ers are usually comprised of more than one ampliï¬cation stage, at least
one of these six conï¬gurations is contained as an ampliï¬cation stage in nearly every ampliï¬er.
î€€e properties derived here form a solid foundation for the analysis and design of multistage
ampliï¬ers presented in later chapters.
5.1
REVIEW OF TWO-PORT NETWORK BASICS
Electronic ampliï¬ers are a subset of the system class commonly identiï¬ed as two-port networks. In
a two-port electronic network, signals are fed into a pair of terminals, ampliï¬ed and/or modiï¬ed
by the system, and ï¬nally extracted at another pair of terminals. Each pair of terminals is identiï¬ed
as a port: signals are fed into an input port and extracted from an output port. î€€e modeling and
analysis of transistor-based ampliï¬ers as well as feedback systemsÂ¹ is greatly simpliï¬ed through
the use of two-port network principles.
Â¹î€€e interconnection of two-port networks as used in the analysis of feedback ampliï¬ers will be discussed in Chapter 8 (Book 2).

302
5. SINGLE TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
î€€e items of interest in an electronic two-port network are the relationships between input
port and output port voltages and currents. î€€ere are a few restrictions, upon which two-port
analysis techniques are based, that must be identiï¬ed:
â€¢ î€€e network must be linear and time invariant.
â€¢ External connections may be made only to port terminals: no external connections can be
made to any node internal to the port.
â€¢ All current entering one terminal of a port must exit the other terminal of that port.
â€¢ Sources and loads must be connected directly across the two terminals of a port.
Given the highly non-linear behavior of transistors, it may seem unusual to attempt to
use two-port network analysis to describe transistor systems. It is possible, under small-signal
conditions, however, to adequately model non-linear systems as incrementally linear. Electronic
circuits previously discussed in this text have shown, along with non-linear operation, regions
of linear operation. It is within these regions of linear operation that two-port analysis proves a
particularly useful technique for the modeling of electronic systems.
As with all electronic systems, it is important to deï¬ne sign conventions: Figure 5.1 is a
representation of a two-port network with appropriate voltage and current polarity deï¬nitions.
As is standard in electronic systems, the voltage and current at each port obey the passive sign
convention. Typically, the input port is identiï¬ed as port #1 and the output port as port #2.Â²
î€€ree-terminal devices, such as transistors, can also be modeled using two-port techniques.
One terminal is selected as a common terminal: that terminal is extended to both of the ports and
becomes the reference (negative) terminal to each port.

Figure 5.1: Two-port network sign conventions.
î€€ere are six basic sets of equivalent descriptive parameters for every two-port network:
1. Impedance parameters (z-parameters)â€”port voltages in terms of port currents.
Â²î€€e numbering of ports is not universal in acceptance. It does, however, allow for simple notation in the description of
the parameters relating currents and voltages. In some electronic two-port descriptions of systems, the numbering system
is replaced by a more descriptive identiï¬cation of parameters. One common example of descriptive notation occurs in h-
parameter modeling of Bipolar Junction Transistors as presented in Section 5.2.

5.1. REVIEW OF TWO-PORT NETWORK BASICS
303
2. Admittance parameters (y-parameters)â€”port currents in terms of port voltages.
3. Hybrid parameters (h-parameters)â€”input voltage and output current in terms of input cur-
rent and output voltage.
4. Hybrid parameters (g-parameters)â€”input current and output voltage in terms of input volt-
age and output current.
5. Transmission parameters (ABCD-parameters)â€”input current and voltage in terms of out-
put current and voltage.
6. Transmission parameters (ABCD-parameters)â€”output current and voltage in terms of in-
put current and voltage.
î€€e ï¬rst four of these sets are of particular interest in the study of electronic feedback
systems, set numbers three and four (hybrid parameters) are often used in the description of
transistor properties, and the last two sets of parameters are particularly useful in the study of
communication transmission systems. A short description of the ï¬rst four two-port parameter
set descriptions follows.

5.2
BJT LOW-FREQUENCY MODELS
In previous sections it has been shown that the Ebers-Moll model is an eï¬€ective model in all
regions of Bipolar Junction Transistor operation. It has also been shown that for large-signal
behavior, a simpliï¬ed set of models can bring additional insight into the operation of an electronic
circuit. î€€ese models are ï¬rst-order approximations to BJT operation, and exhibit some degree
of error. When a BJT is operating in the forward-active region, it is appropriate to improve the
modeling of its operation with a second-order approximation. As in most series approximations to
behavior, second-order eï¬€ects are a linearization, about the ï¬rst-order (in this case, the quiescent)
behavior, and are therefore added to the ï¬rst-order behavior.
In the case of BJT operation, small-signal (often called AC) variations are linearized ap-
proximates to the operation of a transistor about its large-signal (often called DC or quiescent)
behavior. Previous discussions of Bipolar Junction TransistorsÂ³ have shown that the forward-
active region (and, similarly, the inverse-active region) of BJT operation are approximately linear
Â³See Section 3.5 (Book 1)â€”Digital Applications. In particular, discussions of the transfer relationship for the logic inverter
and ECL logic OR gate showed a linear transition region between the two logic levels. î€€ese transition regions occurred when
the BJT was operating in the forward-active region in transition between the cut-oï¬€and saturation regions.

5.2. BJT LOW-FREQUENCY MODELS
309
in nature. It is therefore quite reasonable to assume that small-signal approximations have great
validity in the modeling of BJT operation. î€€e small-signal approximations are made in the form
of approximate models that take the form of two-port networks. Two-port network characteri-
zations are valid for non-linear systems that have regions of linearity: the BJT operating in either
of its active regions is such a system.
î€€e ï¬rst obvious problem in modeling a BJT as a two-port is that there are only three ter-
minals rather than the four that seem to be necessary. î€€is obstacle is overcome by assigning one
BJT terminal common to both ports. Practice has made two possibilities for this common terminal
standard: either the emitter or the base are chosen to be the common terminal. In Figure 5.3, an
npn BJT is shown as a two-port network with the emitter terminal chosen as common. In Chap-
ter 3 (Book 1), it was shown that the major controlling port of a BJT is the base-emitter junction.
î€€us, the port containing the base-emitter junction is considered the input port and the remain-
ing port (which contains the collector terminal and the common terminal), the output port. Since
the terminal voltages and currents for BJTs have previously been identiï¬ed with subscripts that
are descriptive rather than the general numerical form of Section 5.1, the descriptive subscripts
will be continued in the BJT two-port representation.
  ic
C
ib
C
vCF
vDF
Figure 5.3: A bipolar junction transistor as a two-port network (emitter as common terminal).
î€€e modeling process for transistor circuit performance is:
1. Model the BJT with an appropriate DC model.
2. Determine the circuit quiescent (DC) conditionsâ€”verify BJT active region.
3. Determine the BJT AC model parameters from the quiescent conditions.
4. Create an AC equivalent circuit.
5. Determine the circuit AC performance by:
(a) replacing the BJT by its AC model, or
(b) using previously derived results for the circuit topology.
6. Add the results of the DC and AC analysis to obtain total circuit performance.

310
5. SINGLE TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS

have been discussed thoroughly in Chapter 3 (Book 1): discussions of AC modeling follow. î€€e
steps relating to circuit performance are discussed in later sections of this chapter.

5.3
COMMON-EMITTER AMPLIFIERS
A description of the general performance of a common-emitter ampliï¬er can be derived directly
from the voltage transfer relationship of a BJT logic inverter. î€€e BJT logic inverter, discussed in
Section 3.5 (Book 1), has two distinct regions where the voltage output is relatively constant: when
the BJT is in either the saturation or in cut-oï¬€region. Between these two regions lies a linear
region where the variation in the output voltage about the quiescent point is directly proportional
to the variation in the input voltage. î€€is linear-region proportionality constant for the logic
inverter was seen in Example 3.5-1 to be negative and greater than 1 in magnitude: the input
signal variations were ampliï¬ed and inverted. î€€e discussions of Section 3.5 (Book 1) were based
on coarse, ï¬rst-order approximations of the operation of a BJT in each of its regions of operation.
î€€is section develops the characteristics of this type of ampliï¬er (and its close relatives) using the
second-order h-parameter approximations developed earlier in this chapter.
Common-emitter ampliï¬ers have the same general circuit topology as the logic inverter:
â€¢ the input signal enters the BJT at the base,
â€¢ the output signal exits the BJT at the collector, and
â€¢ the emitter is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal, some-
times with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-emitter ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.8. It is necessary that the qui-
escent point of the BJT be set with the circuitry external to the transistor so that it is in the
forward-active region. î€€e values of the resistors, Rc and Rs, and the DC voltage sources, VCC

5.3. COMMON-EMITTER AMPLIFIERS

and VBB, have therefore been chosen so that the BJT is in the forward-active region and the circuit
will operate as an ampliï¬er. î€€e voltage source, vs, is a small-signal AC source.
Once the circuit quiescent (vs D 0) conditions have been calculated and it has been deter-
mined that the BJT is in the forward-active region of operation, the signiï¬cant h-parameters can
be calculated to form the small-signal model of the transistor:

î€€e small-signal (often called AC) circuit performance can now be calculated. Total circuit per-
formance is the sum of quiescent and small-signal performance: the process is basically a su-
perposition of the zero-frequency solution with the low-frequency AC solution.â¹ In each case,
solutions are formed by setting the independent sources at all other frequencies to zero. AC cir-
cuit performance is obtained through analysis of a circuit obtained by setting the original circuit
DC sources to zero, and then replacing the BJT with its h-parameter two-port model. î€€is circuit
reconï¬guration process, applied to Figure 5.8, is shown in Figure 5.9.
î€€e small-signal performance characteristicsÂ¹â° that are of interest in any ampliï¬er are: the
current gain, the input resistance, the voltage gain, and the output resistance. For the simple common-
emitter ampliï¬er under consideration, these characteristics can be obtained from analysis of the
circuit of Figure 5.9b. Deï¬nitions for these quantities often vary due to diï¬€ering deï¬nition of the
exact location of the point of measurement. Care must always be taken to ensure that measurement
points are clearly understood before any analysis begins.

î€€e modeling process for transistor circuit performance was described in Section 5.2 to
contain the following steps:
1. Model the BJT with an appropriate DC model.
2. Determine the circuit quiescent (DC) conditionsâ€”verify BJT active region.

4. Create an AC equivalent circuit.

5.3.1
COMMON-EMITTER AMPLIFIERS WITH NON-ZERO EMITTER
RESISTANCE
Several of the characteristics of a common-emitter ampliï¬er can be altered through the addition
of a resistor connected between the emitter and ground.
It has previously been shown in Section 3.7 (Book 1) that the addition of such a resistor
greatly improves bias stability due to BJT variations. î€€e addition of an emitter resistor has the
following eï¬€ects on common-emitter ampliï¬ers:
â€¢ increased input resistance,
â€¢ increased output resistance,
â€¢ decreased voltage gain, and
â€¢ unchanged current gain.
Derivation of these eï¬€ects follows the process described in Section 5.2.
In order to determine the AC performance of this ampliï¬er, the quiescent conditions must
ï¬rst be obtained. If the BJT is operating in the forward-active region, an AC equivalent model
of the circuit can be obtained (Figure 5.12a) and the BJT can be replaced by its h-parameter
modelÂ¹Â¹ (based on the quiescent conditions) in the AC equivalent circuit (Figure 5.12b). Circuit
performance parameters can now be obtained from this equivalent circuit.
Â¹Â¹î€€e initial calculations assume that hoe is small and can be ignored. Calculations to determine the eï¬€ect of non-zero hoe
follow in Subsection 5.3.2.

5.3.2
THE EFFECT OF NON-ZERO hoe ON COMMON-EMITTER
AMPLIFIERS WITH AN EMITTER RESISTOR
As with simple common-emitter ampliï¬ers, the eï¬€ects of non-zero hoe can only be seen when the
collector resistance becomes large: when Rc approaches or exceeds ro in magnitude. With simple
common-emitter ampliï¬ers, the most noticeable eï¬€ect of non-zero hoe is on the output resistance
of the ampliï¬er. Input resistance is unaï¬€ected, while the current and voltage gain are limited if
the external collector resistance, Rc, is large. Non-zero hoe in the presence of an emitter resistor
eï¬€ects all the common-emitter ampliï¬er characteristics in a complex fashion.

5.3.3
COUPLING AND BYPASS CAPACITORS
Often the demands of properly biasing a BJT into the forward-active region and applying an AC
signal to the input create conï¬‚icting circuit topology requirements. It is not always possible to put
the AC input in series with a DC biasing voltage as has been previously described. In addition,
the need for high voltage gain and good bias stability creates a design requirement conï¬‚ict in
the magnitude of the emitter resistor, Re. One possible design alternative that can resolve these
conï¬‚icts involves the use of capacitors to couple the AC signal into the ampliï¬er and/or to bypass
the emitter resistor.
If an AC signal is imposed upon a capacitor, the complex impedance of the capacitor is
given by:
zC D
1
j!C ;
where
C D the capacitance of the capacitor in farads (F)
! D the frequency of the sinusoidal signal in rad/sec.
At DC .! D 0/, the capacitor has inï¬nite impedance: a capacitor blocks DC signals. If
either the frequency or the capacitance is large, the impedance approaches zero or becomes rela-
tively small compared to adjacent circuit resistances. For purposes of demonstration, assume that
a band of frequencies exist for which the impedance of all discrete capacitors in a circuit will be
essentially zero. î€€is band of frequencies is called the midband frequency regionÂ¹Â² of a circuit. If
the AC input signals to a circuit are within the midband frequency region of the circuit, each
discrete capacitor will appear to be the equivalent of a short circuit. Each capacitor will, however,
appear to be the equivalent of an open circuit to the bias (DC) circuitry.Â¹Â³
In the presence of coupling and/or bypass capacitors, the modeling of ampliï¬er circuit
performance is slightly altered. In determining quiescent conditions, each capacitor is replaced by
an open circuit in addition to replacing the BJT with an appropriate model. î€€e AC equivalent
circuit is drawn with each capacitors replaced by a short.

Ë‡F D 150
and

Â¹Â²Often called the midband region.
Â¹Â³î€€e transition as frequency increases between an apparent open circuit and an apparent short circuit is beyond these early
discussions. It is extensively covered in Section IIIâ€”Frequency Dependence.

5.4
COMMON-COLLECTOR AMPLIFIERS
Common-collector ampliï¬ers have the following general circuit topology:
â€¢ the input signal enters the BJT at the base,
â€¢ the output signal exits the BJT at the emitter, and
â€¢ the collector is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal, some-
times with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-collector ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.15. î€€e collector resistor, Rc,
is unnecessary in many applications, it is shown here for generality. î€€e quiescent point of the
BJT must be set with the circuitry external to the transistor so that it is in the forward-active
region. î€€e values of the resistors, Rc and Rb, and the DC voltage sources, VCC and VBB, have
therefore been chosen so that the BJT is in the forward-active region and the circuit will operate
as an ampliï¬er.

to form the small-signal model of the transistor:

5.5
COMMON-BASE AMPLIFIERS
Common-base ampliï¬ers have the following general circuit topology:
â€¢ the input signal enters the BJT at the emitter,
â€¢ the output signal exits the BJT at the collector, and
â€¢ the base is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal, sometimes
with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-base ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.20. î€€e quiescent point of the BJT must
be set with the circuitry external to the transistor so that it is in the forward-active region. î€€e
values of the resistors, Rc, the base resistors, Rb1 and Rb2, and the DC voltage sources, VCC and
VBB, have therefore been chosen so that the BJT is in the forward-active region and the circuit
will operate as an ampliï¬er.

5.6
COMPARISON OF BJT AMPLIFIER TYPES
Single-BJT ampliï¬ers have been shown to fall into three general categories: Common-Emitter
(both with and without an emitter resistor), Common-Collector, and Common-Base. î€€e per-
formance characteristics for each type of ampliï¬er are summarized in Table 5.6.
Table 5.6: Qualitative comparison of BJT ampliï¬er conï¬gurations

î€€e common-emitter conï¬guration appears the most useful of the three types: it provides
both signiï¬cant current and voltage gain (in each case with an inversion).
î€€e common-emitter ampliï¬er input and output impedances are high. In fact, this con-
ï¬guration is the most versatile of the three types and will often form the major gain portion of
multiple-transistor ampliï¬ers.
î€€e common-collector and common-base conï¬gurations amplify either current or volt-
age, but not both. Neither inverts either the voltage or current signal. î€€ese conï¬gurations ï¬nd
greatest utility as impedance-matching (or buï¬€er) stages of multi-stage ampliï¬ers. Common-
collector ampliï¬er stages are capable of easily driving low-impedance loads. Common-base stages
can impedance-match a very low-impedance source.
Quantitative expressions for the ampliï¬er performance characteristics are found in Ta-
ble 5.7. î€€ese expressions are based on the often realistic assumption concerning the relative
size of the external resistors (connected to the collector and emitter terminals) and the BJT out-
put resistance: hoe.Re C Rc/  1. High resistance loads require the more exact expressions for
ampliï¬er performance that do not depend on that assumption. î€€ese expressions can be found in
previous subsections concerning the eï¬€ects of non-zero hoe.

5.8
COMMON-SOURCE AMPLIFIERS
Between the ohmic and cut-oï¬€regions of the FET characteristics lies a linear region where the
variation in the output voltage about the quiescent point is directly proportional to the variation
in the input voltage. î€€is section develops the characteristics of the common-source ampliï¬er
using the small-signal parameters developed in Section 5.7.
Common-source ampliï¬ers have the following general circuit topology:
â€¢ the input signal enters the FET at the gate,
â€¢ the output signal exits the FET at the drain, and
â€¢ the source terminal is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal,
sometimes with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-source ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.27. Although a enhancement
NMOSFET ampliï¬er is shown, the small-signal analysis of the ampliï¬er is valid for all types
of FETs. As in BJT ampliï¬ers, the quiescent point of the FET must be set with circuitry external
to the transistor so that it is in the FET saturation region. î€€e values of the resistors, RD and
RG, and the DC voltage sources, VGG and VDD, have therefore been chosen so that the FET is in
the saturation region and the circuit will operate as an ampliï¬er. Since the gate current is zero,
VGG D VG and the gate to source voltage, VGS, must have a value that places the operation of the
FET in the saturation region. î€€e voltage source, vS, is a small-signal AC source with source
resistance RS.
One the quiescent conditions (vs D 0) have been calculated, and it has been determined
that the FET is in the saturation region, the signiï¬cant small-signal conductances can be calcu-
lated by referring to Table 5.8.

5.8.1
COMMON-SOURCE AMPLIFIERS WITH NON-ZERO SOURCE
RESISTANCE
Several characteristics of the common-source ampliï¬er (Figure 5.30) can be altered through the
addition of a resistor connected between the source and common (ground). î€€e source resistor
has the following eï¬€ects on common-source ampliï¬ers:
â€¢ increased output resistance
â€¢ decreased voltage gain
Derivation of these eï¬€ects follows the process described in Section 5.7.
In order to determine the AC performance of this ampliï¬er, the quiescent conditions must
ï¬rst be obtained. If the FET is operating in the saturation region, an AC equivalent model of
the circuit can be obtained (Figure 5.31a) and the FET can be replaced by its small-signal model
(based on the quiescent conditions) in the AC equivalent circuit (Figure 5.31b). Circuit perfor-
mance can then be obtained from this equivalent circuit.
Input Resistance
î€€e input resistance (shown in Figure 5.31b) is the same as the input resistance for a common-
source ampliï¬er without a source resistor,
Ri D 1:

5.9. COMMON-DRAIN AMPLIFIERS

5.9
COMMON-DRAIN AMPLIFIERS
Common-drain ampliï¬ers have the following general circuit topology:
â€¢ the input signal enters the FET at the gate,
â€¢ the output signal exits the FET at the source, and
â€¢ the drain terminal is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal,
sometimes with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-drain ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.34. Although Figure 5.34 shows a
common-drain n-JFET ampliï¬er, the small-signal analysis of the ampliï¬er is valid for all types of
FETs. As in BJT ampliï¬ers, the quiescent point of the FET must be set with circuitry external to
the transistor insuring linear operation which for the FET is the saturation region. î€€e values of
the resistors, RD and RG, and the DC voltage sources, VGG and VDD, have therefore been chosen
so that the FET is in the saturation region and the circuit will operate as an ampliï¬er. Since the
gate current is zero, VGG D VG and the gate to source voltage, VGS, must have a value that places
the operation of the FET in the saturation region. î€€e voltage source, vS, is a small-signal AC
source with source resistance RS.

5.10
COMMON-GATE AMPLIFIERS
Common-gate ampliï¬ers have the following general circuit topology:
â€¢ the input signal enters the FET at the source,
â€¢ the output signal exits the FET at the drain, and
â€¢ the gate terminal is connected to a constant voltage, often the ground (common) terminal,
sometimes with an intervening resistor.
A simple common-gate ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 5.38. Although Figure 5.38 shows a
common-gate depletion NMOSFET ampliï¬er, the small-signal analysis of the ampliï¬er is valid
for all types of FETs. As in BJT ampliï¬ers, the quiescent point of the FET must be set with
circuitry external to the transistor so that it is in the FET saturation region. î€€e values of the
resistors, RD and RG, and the DC voltage sources, VGG and VDD, have therefore been chosen so
that the FET is in the saturation region and the circuit will operate as an ampliï¬er. Since the
gate current is zero, VGG D VG and the gate to source voltage, VGS, must have a value that places
the operation of the FET in the saturation region. î€€e voltage source, vS, is a small-signal AC
source with source resistance RS. Once the quiescent conditions (vs D 0) have been calculated,
and it has been determined that the FET is in the saturation region, the signiï¬cant small-signal
conductances can be calculated by referring to Table 5.8.
For the depletion NMOSFET,

5.11
COMPARISON OF FET AMPLIFIER TYPES
Single FET ampliï¬ers have been shown to fall into three general categories: Common-Source
(both with and without a source resistor), Common-Collector, and Common-Base. î€€e perfor-
mance characteristics for each type of ampliï¬er are summarized in Table 5.12.
Table 5.12: Qualitative comparison of FET ampliï¬er conï¬gurations
$4

$%

7FSZ )JHI

-PX

)JHI
.FEJVN
 6OJUZ
.FEJVN UP )JHI

)JHI
7FSZ )JHI
-PX
)JHI
î€€e common-source conï¬guration appears the most useful of the three types: it provides
signiï¬cant voltage gain with inversion. î€€e common-source ampliï¬er input and output resistances
are high. In fact, this conï¬guration is the most versatile of the three types and will often form the
major voltage gain stage portion of multiple-transistor ampliï¬ers.
î€€e common-drain conï¬guration is, for all practical purposes, a unity gain, non-inverting
buï¬€er ampliï¬er for impedance matching between electronic circuit stages. Common-drain am-
pliï¬ers are capable of easily driving low resistance loads. Common-gate ampliï¬ers can act as an
impedance matching stage from low to high resistance electronic circuit stages.
Quantitative expressions for the ampliï¬er performance are found in Table 5.13.

5.12. BIASING TO ACHIEVE MAXIMUM SYMMETRICAL SWING
393
5.12
BIASING TO ACHIEVE MAXIMUM SYMMETRICAL
SWING
Ampliï¬cation can be restricted by the size and positioning of the available undistorted output
swing. Ampliï¬ers consisting strictly of a transistor and resistors can be biased so that the transistor
quiescent point lies in the middle of the linear output voltage range provided by the power supply.
î€€is location of the Q-point allows symmetrical excursion of signal voltages about a central value:
distortion (ampliï¬er saturation) will occur for equal magnitude excursions in the positive and
negative directions.
Ampliï¬ers that have capacitively coupled loads or resistors that are shunted by capacitors in
the output voltage/current relationship do not have the entire range of the power supply for output
voltage swing. Placing the Q-point in the middle of the power supply rails will not provide equal
output voltage swing and will greatly limit the utility of the ampliï¬er. It is therefore important
to be able to easily choose a Q-point that will provide the maximum symmetrical swing for the
ampliï¬er given the various design constraints.
A technique that analytically determines the Q-point for maximum symmetrical swing is
based on transistor output load lines. î€€e output curve for a transistor (either BJT or FET) is
typically given as a current vs. voltage curve. On this curve DC and AC load lines can be drawn
as shown in Figure 5.43.

Figure 5.43: Typical ampliï¬er AC and DC load lines.
î€€e two load lines always intersect at the quiescent point: if there is zero AC, the output
must be on the Q-point of the DC load line. It is also true that the magnitude of the midband AC
load line for all practical ampliï¬er circuits is greater than that of the DC load line. î€€e increased
magnitude AC load line slope decreases the available oscillatory swing along the abscissa to a value
often signiï¬cantly less than the power supply limits. î€€e maximum supply limit {S} is typically

the magnitude of the power supply: the minimum supply limit {C} is determined by the edge of
the linear region of the transistor (for a BJT it is approximately VCE.sat/).
For maximum symmetrical swing it is necessary to choose a Q-point that allows equal
oscillatory space in both the positive and negative directions. A choice either to the right or
left of this optimum value will decrease the symmetrical output voltage swing capability of the
ampliï¬er. In Figure 5.43, the Q-point is chosen so that:

5.13
CONCLUDING REMARKS
î€€e small-signal model of the BJT was developed in this chapter using two-port network analy-
sis and the Ebers-Moll transistor model. î€€ree conï¬gurations of BJT ampliï¬ers were described:
common-emitter, common-collector, and common-base. î€€e current gain, input resistance, out-
put resistance, and voltage gain o feach of the conï¬gurations were found.
î€€e FET small-signal model was also developed three conï¬gurations of FET ampliï¬ers
were described: common-source, common-drain, and common-gate. î€€e input resistance, voltage
gain, and output resistance were found for each conï¬guration.
î€€e modeling process for transistor circuit performance contains the following steps:
1. Determine the quiescent (DC) conditions. Verify that the BJT is in the forward-active
region or the FET is in the saturation region.
2. Determine the transistor small-signal parameters from the quiescent conditions.
3. Create the AC equivalent circuit.
4. Determine the AC performance by replacing the transistor by its small-signal model.
5. Add the results of the CD and AC analysis to obtain total circuit performance.

398
5. SINGLE TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
Restrictions on ampliï¬cation were shown to be dependent on transistor quiescent condi-
tions. A technique that analytically determines the Q point for maximum symmetrical swing
based on load lines was introduced.
Summary Design Example
Whenever the physical distance between a signal source and the load are more than a very small
fraction of a wavelength, the wires connecting the source and load appear to act as a transmission
line. î€€e voltage and current waveforms traveling on a transmission have amplitudes related by the
characteristic impedance of the transmission line, Zo. î€€is characteristic impedance is a function
of the geometry of the transmission line and the dielectric material surrounding the line.
î€€e interfaces between the source, transmission line, and the load must be carefully con-
trolled in order to avoid signal reï¬‚ections: signal reï¬‚ections produce an â€œechoâ€ eï¬€ect. In order to
ensure that there are no signal reï¬‚ections at the source end or the load end of the transmission
line, the output resistance of the source and the input resistance of the load must be equal to the
characteristic impedance of the transmission line.
Design an ampliï¬er that will accept an input signal from a matched 50  transmission line
system. î€€e ampliï¬er will have a voltage gain of Ë™10.
Solution:
In order to have no reï¬‚ections at the load, the input resistance of the ampliï¬er must be
50 . î€€e only ampliï¬er topologies that have small input resistance while maintaining a voltage
gain are the common-base and common-gate conï¬gurations. Of these two, the common-base
conï¬guration is much more common: the common-base conï¬guration is chosen for this design.
Assume typical transistor parameters:
Ë‡F D 150;
VA D 160:
î€€e basic topology of the common-base receiving circuit is shown below. Note that the source is an
AC source with an output resistance matched to the characteristic impedance of the transmission
line.

C H A P T E R
6
Multiple-Transistor Ampliï¬ers
Previous discussions of transistor ampliï¬ers have shown that single transistor ampliï¬ers have a
wide range of possible design characteristics described by the principal performance characteris-
tics: voltage and current gain; input and output resistance. î€€ere are, however, many circumstances
when the desired overall performance characteristics for an ampliï¬er cannot be met by a single
transistor ampliï¬er. î€€at is, the required combination of ampliï¬cation, input resistance, and out-
put resistance may be beyond the capability of a single transistor ampliï¬er. In these circumstances
it is necessary to employ ampliï¬ers that use more than a single transistor.
î€€e obvious approach to changing ampliï¬er performance characteristics is to cascade (con-
nect the output of a given stage directly into the input of the following stage) single transistor am-
pliï¬ers. In this manner, additional gain or the modiï¬cation of input/output resistance can be easily
accomplished. î€€is simple approach to multiple transistor ampliï¬ers is discussed in Section 6.1. A
major drawback to this approach is the relatively high number of electronic components necessary
to accomplish a design. In addition, if the simple stage are coupled with capacitors (a common
practice to decouple the DC bias conditions of each stage), the low frequency performance of the
ampliï¬er can be severely degraded.
Several transistor cascades are commonly packaged as a single unit. Most common among
these cascade types are the Darlington conï¬gurations. î€€ese cascade conï¬gurations have the ad-
vantage of simple external bias circuitry, high performance, and DC coupling of stages. Analysis
of these types is discussed in Section 6.2.
Diï¬€erential ampliï¬ers are another type of high-performance multiple transistor ampliï¬er.
î€€ey commonly are based on the high gain of emitter-coupled and source-coupled transistor
ampliï¬ers. Operational ampliï¬ers are a common form of diï¬€erential ampliï¬er that typically have
emitter-coupled or source-coupled ampliï¬ers as input stages.
In addition, the practice of biasing transistor ampliï¬ers with transistor current sources will
be explored in this Chapter. î€€is bias technique is particularly suited to integrated ampliï¬ers con-
tained on a single semiconductor chip where transistors are more practical and economic devices
than resistors.
Active (as opposed to resistive) loads will be explored as a means of increasing gain and
modifying output resistance without the use of extremely large resistors and power supplies.

424
6. MULTIPLE-TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
6.1
MULTISTAGE AMPS USING SIMPLE STAGES
CASCADED
Among the many multistage connections possible in large ampliï¬ers, the cascade connection is
the most simple. In a cascade connection, the output voltage and current of an ampliï¬er stage are
passed directly to the input of the next ampliï¬er stage. A two-stage cascade-connected ampliï¬er
is represented by Figure 6.1. In this ampliï¬er, the outputs, vo1 and io1, of the ï¬rst ampliï¬er stage
become the inputs, vi2 and ii2, to the second stage without any modiï¬cation. For the discussions
in this section, it is assumed that each of the ampliï¬er stages consists of one of the simple single-
transistor ampliï¬ers described in Chapter 5.Â¹

î€€e determination of the performance of multistage ampliï¬ers follows the same basic steps
that were derived in Chapter 5:

426
6. MULTIPLE-TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
1 Model the transistors with an appropriate DC model (circuit or analytic).
2 Determine the circuit quiescent (DC) conditions - verify active region for BJTs or saturation
region for FETs.
3 Determine the transistor AC model parameters from the quiescent conditions.
4 Create an AC equivalent circuit.
5 Determine the AC performance of each ampliï¬er stage by:
(a) replacing the transistors by their respective AC models, or
(b) using previously derived results for the circuit topology.
5a Combine the stage performance quantities to obtain total ampliï¬er performance.
6 Add the results of the DC and AC analysis to obtain total circuit performance.
Only the ï¬fth step has been modiï¬ed to reï¬‚ect the new condition that several stages may
comprise the total ampliï¬er.

All required ampliï¬er performance characteristics have been calculated: the problem statement

While the capacitive coupling of ampliï¬er stages has its primary advantage in the decou-
pling of the individual stage quiescent conditions, there are disadvantages that must be considered.
î€€e addition of capacitors may seriously degrade the low-frequency response of an ampliï¬er. In
addition, the decoupling of quiescent conditions necessitates the individual biasing of each tran-
sistor into the forward-active (for BJTs) or saturation (for FETs) regions. Individual biasing can
signiï¬cantly increase the numbers of bias elements (in discrete ampliï¬ers these elements are usu-
ally resistors) which will increase the size, cost, and power consumption of an ampliï¬er. It is
therefore advantageous, whenever possible, to directly couple ampliï¬er stages. Capacitive cou-
pling of the input source and the load is often unavoidable due to the DC oï¬€sets often necessary
in these simple ampliï¬er stages.
An example of a two-stage cascade-connected ampliï¬er with directly-coupled stages is
shown in Figure 6.2. î€€e bias for the second transistor stage (the BJT for this ampliï¬er) is depen-
dent on the quiescent conditions of the ï¬rst transistor stage. î€€e choice to use dependent biasing

6.1. MULTISTAGE AMPS USING SIMPLE STAGES CASCADED
429
rather than capacitively couple independent stages has eliminated, in this case, two bias resistors
and a capacitor from the design.
Under certain circumstances, is might be possible to eliminate other elements from this
ampliï¬er. î€€e purpose of Rg is to ensure that the quiescent voltage at the gate of Q1 is at zero
potential. If the designer is absolutely sure the input source, vs, has no DC bias, the resistor Rg
and its associated input capacitor could also be eliminated from this design.

6.1.1
DESIGN CHOICES FOR TRANSISTOR CONFIGURATION IN A
CASCADE-CONNECTED AMPLIFIER
î€€e previous example derivations and calculate on of voltage and current gain and input and out-
put resistance apply to any conï¬guration of cascade-connected ampliï¬er. As each stage is added,
the gain expression is increased by only one additional term. î€€e individual stages may be any
of the three BJT or three FET conï¬gurations and any number of these stages may be connected
in any order. î€€ere are, however, design choices that are better engineering practice than other
choices.
î€€e basic reason for choosing a multiple-stage ampliï¬er over a single-stage ampliï¬er are
one or more of the following performance characteristics:
â€¢ increased ampliï¬cation
â€¢ input impedance modiï¬cation
â€¢ output impedance modiï¬cation
Common-emitter and common-source ampliï¬er stages are ideal for increased ampliï¬cation pur-
poses. Each type exhibits signiï¬cant voltage gain and signiï¬cant current gain. In addition, when

6.2. DARLINGTON AND OTHER SIMILAR CONFIGURATIONS
433
cascade-connected, the relatively high input resistance of these stage conï¬gurations does not sig-
niï¬cantly load previous ampliï¬cation stages (voltage gain is a function of the load). Common-base
and common-gate ampliï¬ers exhibit good voltage gain but no current gain. î€€e relatively low in-
put resistance of these stages also produces a gain-reducing load to previous stages. Consequently,
these stages are most useful as low input resistance ï¬rst stages. Common-collector and common-
drain ampliï¬ers have good current gain but no voltage gain. î€€eir relatively high input resistance
allows them to follow a ampliï¬cation stage without seriously decreasing the voltage gain. î€€ese
two conï¬gurations are most useful for low output resistance ï¬nal stages. In summary, the design
choices for a cascade-connected ampliï¬er are usually are based on the following principles:
â€¢ First stage should not be common-collector or common-drain
(a) Low input resistance cases: common-base or common-gate
(b) Other cases: common-emitter or common-source
â€¢ Intermediate stages should be common-emitter or common-source
â€¢ Final stage should not be common-base or common-gate
(a) Low output resistance cases: common-collector or common-drain
(b) Other cases: common-emitter or common-source
Special purpose ampliï¬ers may violate these design principles as seen in Section 6.2.
6.2
DARLINGTON AND OTHER SIMILAR
CONFIGURATIONS
In addition to cascade-connected single-transistor ampliï¬ers, there exist several two-transistor
conï¬gurations that are commonly analyzed and often packaged as signal stages. î€€ese particular
combinations are unusual in that they seem to violate to conï¬guration principles of good ampliï¬er
design as itemized in the last section. î€€e conï¬gurations do, however, produce ampliï¬ers with
speciï¬c, signiï¬cantly-enhanced properties.
Most common among these conï¬gurations are the two Darlington BJT conï¬gurations and
the Cascode conï¬guration. Mixed BJT and FET combinations are currently not as common
as the single-type conï¬gurations. î€€e BiFET (BiCMOS) Darlington seems most promising of
these newer technology combinations.
6.2.1
THE DARLINGTON CONFIGURATIONS
î€€e Darlington two-transistor ampliï¬er conï¬gurations consist of a pair of BJTs connected so that
the emitter of the input transistor couples directly, in an AC sense, into the base of the output
transistor. A DC biasing current source is also commonly present at this transistor interconnection
node. î€€e output is taken at either the collector or the emitter of the second transistor. î€€us,

434
6. MULTIPLE-TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
in a Darlington connected BJT pair, the ï¬rst transistor stage is a common-collector while the
second stage can be either common-collector or common-emitter. Figure 6.3 shows the two basic
Darlington conï¬gurations.Â³
î€€e Darlington Conï¬gurations incorporate an additional transistor so that overall circuit
performance is altered signiï¬cantly:
â€¢ Current Gain is Increased, and
â€¢ Input Resistance is Increased.
î€€ese increases in current gain and input resistance are by a factor of approximately Ë‡F . î€€e exact
change in circuit performance requires careful analysis.

It is common practice to analyze these two-transistor pairs as a single subcircuit. î€€is sub-
circuit takes on many of the characteristic properties of a single BJT, modiï¬ed due to the presence
of two transistors. î€€e subcircuit nodes that correspond to the so called â€œcomposite transistorâ€ are
labeled in Figure 6.4 as BC; C C and EC.
î€€e fourth terminal in Darlington subcircuits exists solely for DC biasing. Commercially pack-
aged Darlingtons allow for the fourth terminal in several diï¬€erent ways:
â€¢ by providing a separate external connection (a four terminal package),
â€¢ internally providing for the bias current or voltage source with additional circuitry within
the package (a three terminal package), or,
â€¢ in the case of the bias current source, occasionally eliminating it entirely (a three terminal
package). î€€is case is equivalent to setting the bias current source, IBias, to zero value.
Â³Other slight variations occur. î€€ese variations are due a variety of possible connections for the voltage and current biasing
sources, VCC and IBias.

6.2.2
THE DUAL COMMON-COLLECTOR DARLINGTON
CONFIGURATION
î€€e Dual Common-Collector Darlington Conï¬guration (Figure 6.4) is the most common of the
two Darlington Conï¬gurations: a wide variety of commercial packages of this conï¬guration of
transistors is available. In this conï¬guration, the base of the composite transistor is the base of the
ï¬rst BJT (Q1), the two BJT collectors are connected together to form the composite collector,
and the emitter of the second BJT (Q2) forms the composite emitter. As one might expect, the
composite transistor can operate in all four regions that are usually associated with BJTs: the cut-
oï¬€, saturation, forward-active, and inverse-active regions. Simpliï¬ed models, and their equivalent
circuits, of composite transistor operation in each region are of the same form as those developed
for BJTs in Section 3.4 (Book 1). However, the composite transistor will have characteristic pa-
rameters in each of these regions that vary from typical BJT values.
In the cut-oï¬€region, the composite transistor operates as three terminals with open circuits
between. In order to turn the subcircuit on, both BJT base-emitter junctions must become forward
biased. Since the composite base-emitter consists of two BJT base-emitter junctions, the typical
turn-on base-emitter voltage for this composite transistorâ´ is approximately double that of a single
BJT:
VBE.on/  1:2 V:

modeled by two ON BJTs. î€€us, in the saturation region,
VBE.sat/  1:6 V:

are discussed in Chapter 3 (Book 1).

A FET variation of the cascode conï¬guration is often found in analog design. î€€e FET
cascode consists of a Common-Sourceâ€”Common Gate connection. As one might expect, the
only major change over a single common-source stage is increased output impedance. Since the
ampliï¬cation of a common-source stage is highly dependent on the output impedance, rd, of the
FET, the FET cascode will have greatly improved voltage gain. One drawback is that maximum
symmetrical swing may be degraded with the FET cascode.
6.2.5
THE BIFET DARLINGTON CONFIGURATION
No useful all-FET Darlington circuits exist: the potential for increased input impedance and
current gain have little signiï¬cance in FET circuitry. î€€ere is, however, a useful Darlington circuit
connection that combines the properties of FETs and BJTs. In this conï¬guration the input BJT
of a Darlington pair is replaced by a FET (Figure 6.10). î€€e connection retains the near-inï¬nite
input impedance of the FET and, with the addition of the BJT, attains a larger eï¬€ective composite
transconductance, gm and a lower output resistance, rd. Common technologies currently in use

6.3
EMITTER-COUPLED AND SOURCE-COUPLED PAIRS
Emitter-coupled and source-coupled pairs of transistors are widely used to form the basic el-
ement of diï¬€erential ampliï¬ers with the most common application being the input stage of an
Operational Ampliï¬er. î€€e output of such ampliï¬er stages is proportional to the diï¬€erence in the
input voltages and has a high Common-Mode Rejection Ratio. High CMRR allows the input
to be diï¬€erential or single (one input at a ï¬xed voltage). î€€e output can be similarly diï¬€erential
or single (as in the OpAmp).
Another useful property of emitter-coupled and source-coupled ampliï¬er stages is that they
can be cascaded without interstage coupling capacitors: any common-mode DC oï¬€set that may
be present has a negligible eï¬€ect on the performance of any stage. Input impedance is typically
high for FET pairs and moderate for BJT pairs: output impedance is moderate for all types.
6.3.1
EMITTER-COUPLED PAIRS
î€€e basic topology for an emitter-coupled pair is shown in Figure 6.11. In order to form a good
diï¬€erential ampliï¬er, the circuit must by a symmetric network: the transistors must have similar
properties and corresponding collector resistances should have identical values (Rc1 D Rc2 D
Rc). î€€ese collector resistances are often discrete resistors; in integrated circuit applications, they
are more often active loads.â¹ In integrated circuit realizations of an emitter-coupled pair, the
biasing network is typically in the form of a transistor current source; in other realizations it can
be as simple as a single resistor. For generality, the biasing network is shown in its most general
form as a Norton equivalent source.
î€€e topology of this ampliï¬er is very similar to that of the input section of an Emitter-
Coupled Logic gate. Previous explorations of that logic gate familyÂ¹â° found that a linear region
of ampliï¬cation exists in the transition region between the two logic levels. î€€is region extends
over a very small range of input voltage diï¬€erences: the diï¬€erence between the voltages at the two
BJT base terminals can not exceed a few tenths of a volt before one of the transistors enters the
cut-oï¬€region and the ampliï¬er saturates. Still, as has been seen in the case of the OpAmp, it is
not necessary for the input voltage diï¬€erence to be large in order to create a highly useful device.
â¹Active loads are discussed in Section 6.5.
Â¹â°Emitter-Coupled Logic gates are discussed in Section 3.5 (Book 1).

DC Analysis of the Source-Coupled Pair
DC analysis of FET circuits is inherently more complicated than BJT circuits due to the highly
non-linear large-signal behavior of FETs. î€€us, a closed-form expression for the quiescent FET
drain current is not easily derived. If, however, the circuit is truly symmetric, the DC analysis can
be accomplished through the use of a â€œhalf-circuitâ€ as shown in Figure 6.14.
Half-circuit analysis uses the property that the drain currents for the two FETs are identical:
thus the eï¬€ect of the Norton source on one of the FETs appears to be modiï¬ed. î€€is modiï¬cation
changes the apparent Norton source: the value of the Norton current is halved and the Norton
resistance appears to be doubled. î€€e drain current and gate-source voltage for this single FET
quiescent equivalent circuit can be obtained using the half-circuit quiescent equivalent and the
techniques outlined in Chapter 4 (Book 1). î€€ese derived quiescent conditions and the FET

î€€e AC equivalent circuit for zero vi2 is shown in Figure 6.15.
î€€e gain path to vo1 is a common-source (with a source resistor) ampliï¬er stage, while the

analysis of this circuit. Two derived input resistances aid in calculations. î€€e resistance, R2, is the

6.3.3
VARIATIONS ON THE THEME
In an eï¬€ort to improve the various performance characteristics of emitter-coupled and source-
coupled ampliï¬ers, designers often change the circuit topology while keeping its basic character-
istics. Two common techniques for altering circuit performance are:
â€¢ Including a resistor between the emitter or source terminal and the common terminal
â€¢ Using a compound transistor (Darlington, etc.) instead of a single transistor
Additional resistors have the eï¬€ect of increasing the range of input voltages over which the am-
pliï¬er is linear and increases the input resistance of emitter-coupled ampliï¬ers. Compound tran-
sistors can have a variety of eï¬€ects. Figure 6.16 shows a simpliï¬ed schematic of the input stage
of an OpAmp that uses compound Darlington transistors and additional emitter resistors to alter
performance characteristics.
While the circuit of Figure 6.16 indicates a multiple BJT diï¬€erential ampliï¬er, the com-
pound transistors are not limited to BJTs. BiFET and BiCMOS technologies allow for the mix-
ing of transistor types in the diï¬€erential pair as long as the circuit remains symmetric. Analysis of
such ampliï¬ers follows the same basic techniques as have been demonstrated in this section. î€€e
compound transistor parameters rather than single transistor parameters are used and appropri-
ate resistances (R1 and/or R2 in the previous derivations) are modiï¬ed to reï¬‚ect any additional
emitter or source resistors.

6. MULTIPLE-TRANSISTOR AMPLIFIERS
6.4
TRANSISTOR CURRENT SOURCES
Current sources may be used to bias the transistor circuit in the forward active region for a small-
signal ampliï¬er. î€€e biasing schemes explored thus far apply a voltage across the base-emitter
junction of the BJT or the gate-source of the FET. Current source biasing may be used in lieu
of a base or gate resistor bias network to set the quiescent collector or drain current, and is the
preferred method for biasing diï¬€erential ampliï¬ers. Bias networks in integrated circuits most
often depend on current sources.
6.4.1

suï¬ƒciently more positive than the emitter voltage such that the transistor operates in the forward
active region. î€€e constant current is supplied by the collector of the transistor.

6.4.5
WIDLAR CURRENT SOURCE
In the two and three transistor current mirrors, the load current IC2 is approximately equal to
the reference current for large Ë‡F . To supply a load current of milliamperes with power supply
voltages in the order of 10 V, the reference resistor must be in the several thousands of Ohms. î€€is
poses little problem for the designer. However, if the load current is small (in the order of 5 A),
the required reference resistor approaches or exceeds 1 M. In Operational ampliï¬er integrated
circuits, the low input current requirement mandates a low bias current for the input emitter-
coupled diï¬€erential ampliï¬er. A resistor of this magnitude is prohibitive in terms of the area that
it would require on the chip. It is also diï¬ƒcult to fabricate accurate resistance values on integrated
circuit chips.
î€€e load and reference currents can be made to be substantially diï¬€erent by forcing the base-
emitter voltages of Q1 and Q2 to be unequal in the two transistor current mirror of Figure 6.18.
One way to achieve this goal of substantially diï¬€erent load and reference currents is the addition
of an emitter resistor to Q2 as is done in the Widlar current source shown in Figure 6.21.
Applying Kirchhoï¬€â€™s Voltage Law to the base-emitter loop of Q1 and Q2,
0 D VBE1   VBE2 C IE2R2:

6.5
ACTIVE LOADS
In integrated circuit ampliï¬ers, transistor current source conï¬gurations are often used as active
loads. Since small-signal gain in ampliï¬ers is directly proportional to the load resistance, large
loads are desired to achieve large gain. However, increasing the load requires that a large power
supply voltage must be used in order to keep to transistor in the proper region of operation. î€€e
quiescent point will be also be altered. Both of these consequences to the use of a large resistor
for a load are undesirable.
Active loads using current source conï¬gurations are commonly used to provide the high
load required for increasing small-signal gain. î€€e small-signal output resistance of the current
source conï¬guration is used as the load to the amplifying circuit. A Norton and î€€Ã©venin equiv-
alent model of a transistor current source is shown in Figure 6.25.
For the Norton equivalent model, the analysis and design of current sources, discussed in
Section 6.4 using the Ebers-Moll model, is used to determine IO. î€€e output resistance RO is
found by determining the performing small-signal analysis on the current source. Since a tran-
sistor is used in place of a resistor, the load is dependent on both the inherent transistor char-
acteristics and the quiescent point of the circuit. In particular, the Early voltage, VA, is of great
importance when determining the active load of a small-signal transistor ampliï¬er.

6.6
CONCLUDING REMARKS
î€€e range of transistor ampliï¬ers available for use was expanded in this chapter to include the use
of multiple transistor applications. î€€is extension to allows the designer to create ampliï¬ers that
have a combination of ampliï¬cation, input resistance, and output resistance that is not within the
capabilities of single transistor ampliï¬ers.
Cascaded-stage designs, consisting of several simple stages in series, are the dominant form
of multiple transistor ampliï¬ers. Closely related to cascaded-stage designs are the Darlington and
similar grouped-transistor ampliï¬cation stages.
Other common multiple transistor ampliï¬cation stages are based on diï¬€erential inputs
rather than inputs solely referenced to a common terminal. Emitter coupled and source coupled

6.6. CONCLUDING REMARKS
487
transistor pairs form the basis for diï¬€erential ampliï¬ers. Among the many needs for diï¬€erential
inputs are many OpAmp applications.
While the addition of additional ampliï¬cation stages complicates the process, the modeling
and analysis techniques for multiple transistor ampliï¬ers follow the same basic procedure that has
been previously described:
1. Determine the quiescent (DC) conditionsâ€”verify all transistors are in the proper operating
region
2. Determine the small-signal parameters for each transistor from the quiescent conditions
3. Create an AC equivalent circuit
4. Determine the AC performance for the circuit
5. Add the results of the DC and AC analysis to obtain total circuit performance.
Although each step of the procedure may be more complicated, simple steps taken together
provide the desired results.
Integrated circuit ampliï¬er designs are an area where the use of multiple transistors prevails.
In discrete applications resistors are cost eï¬€ective and reliable; in IC applications they create a
multitude of design obstacles. î€€e use of multiple-transistor current sources as ampliï¬er bias
networks solves many of the problems. Current sources can also provide high resistance active
loads for many of the single and multiple transistor ampliï¬er types. Active loads provide high
gain without the use of large resistors or high-voltage sources.
While the interconnection of many transistors in simple ampliï¬ers can satisfy many design
goals, it does not always provide the optimum design. î€€e sensitivity of these designs to transistor
parameter variation is, on occasion, a concern. High power applications also put constraints on
ampliï¬er designs. î€€ese design concerns and several solutions are explored in Chapters 7 and 8.

C H A P T E R
7
Power Ampliï¬ers and Output
Stages
In general, analog ampliï¬ers discussed in the previous chapters are described as small-signal cir-
cuits whose purpose is to increase the amplitude of the input signal or act as an impedance buï¬€er
between other ampliï¬er stages. In reality, these ampliï¬ers accept signals over a broad range of am-
plitudes. î€€is chapter describes ampliï¬ers (or ampliï¬cation stages) that are capable of delivering
high power levels at the output.
Power ampliï¬ers must be capable of delivering a speciï¬c amount of power to a load, such as a
stereo ampliï¬er to a pair of speakers or a radio frequency ampliï¬er to a broadcasting antenna. î€€ey
are also used as output stages in integrated circuits. Because high output powers may be involved,
the eï¬ƒciency of the ampliï¬er to convert a low-power signal to high power becomes increasingly
important. Ineï¬ƒciency causes unwanted increases in transistor operating temperature and may
lead to accelerated device failure. Because power ampliï¬er stages may deliver high output power to
low impedance loads, they are signiï¬cantly diï¬€erent from the low- power small-signal ampliï¬ers.
î€€e large signal nature of power ampliï¬ers warrants special design considerations that may
not be signiï¬cant for small-signal ampliï¬ers. î€€erefore, small-signal approximations and models
either are not appropriate or must be used with care. In large-signal operation, signals transverse
the extremes of the forward-active region of the transistor causing distortion at the output. î€€at
is, when a pure sinusoid is introduced to a power ampliï¬er, the output may no longer be a pure
sinusoid, but a signal composed of the original sinusoid (at some amplitude determined by the
ampliï¬cation) and its Fourier (harmonic) components. î€€e relative amplitude of the fundamental
component decreases with respect to the Fourier components as distortion increases.
Distortion is deï¬ned in one of several ways depending on the particular application of the
circuit. Measures of distortion include:
â€¢ Total harmonic distortion (THD) commonly used for audio circuits,
â€¢ Intermodulation distortion (IMD) using the two-tone ratio method for radio and mi-
crowave frequency circuits,
â€¢ Second- and third-order intercept points also used for radio and microwave circuits,
â€¢ Composite second-order (CSO) and composite third-order beat ratio (CTBR) used for
cable television transmission circuits.

512
7. POWER AMPLIFIERS AND OUTPUT STAGES
Description of the THD and IMD methods will be discussed, and the relationship between the
two measurements will be presented.
î€€e design and analysis concepts introduced in this chapter are:
â€¢ Analysis of distortion
â€¢ Design of large-signal (power) ampliï¬ers with particular interest in reduction of distortion
â€¢ î€€ermal consideration that must be taken into account when designing power ampliï¬ers.
7.1
POWER AMPLIFIER CLASSIFICATION
In previous chapters, ampliï¬ers were classiï¬ed in terms of their circuit conï¬gurations (common-
emitter, common-collector, common-base, common-drain, common-source, and common-gate).
Another classiï¬cation scheme is used when classifying power ampliï¬ers. î€€is scheme labels cir-
cuits according to the portion of the period of the output waveform during which the transistors
conduct. î€€e measure of designation is the conduction angle of each transistor in the circuit,
assuming a sinusoidal input.
Regardless of the classiï¬cation of the power ampliï¬er, it must be capable of handling large
signal amplitudes where the current and voltages swings may be a signiï¬cantly large fraction of
the bias values. In such cases, small-signal analysis and models may not be appropriate and large
signal (DC) transfer characteristics must be used. î€€e limitation on small-signal operation is
discussed in this section.
7.1.1
CLASSIFICATION SCHEME
î€€e ampliï¬er classiï¬cations by conduction angle for sinusoidal inputs are shown in Table 7.1. î€€e
output (collector or drain) current through a transistor for class A, B, AB, and C power ampliï¬ers
are shown in Figure 7.1. In Figure 7.1a, the current ï¬‚ows through the transistor over the whole
period: this is classiï¬ed as Class A operation. In Class B operation, shown in Figure 7.1b, each
transistor only conducts over half the period of the input sinusoidal waveform. Class AB opera-
tion, shown in Figure 7.1c, illustrates current ï¬‚ow through each transistor for greater than a half
cycle but less than the full cycle of the input sinusoid. Figure 7.1d shows Class C operation where
the each transistor conducts over less than half cycle of the input sinusoid. Class D operation is
not shown since the conduction angle could vary with time over the entire cycle.
Initially, it may seem as if Class A operation is the only conï¬guration that will yield low
distortion signals. Class B and class AB power ampliï¬ers assure signal continuity by making
use of arrangements of two transistors that allow each transistor to share portions of the input
signal conduction angle. Class C ampliï¬ers provide single-frequency sinusoidal output by driving
resonant circuits over a small portion of the cycle; the continuity of the sinusoidal output is assured
by the tuned circuit.Â¹ In Class D operation, there are two inputs, the input signal and a sampling
Â¹Class C ampliï¬ers are used for narrow-band signal applications. î€€e focus of this section is on broad-band ampliï¬ers.

the output waveform. In power ampliï¬ers, however, the output signals are large and potentially
distorted. î€€erefore, simple linear small-signal analysis cannot necessarily be used eï¬€ectively. In-

of the power ampliï¬er.

on ampliï¬er distortion speciï¬cations. However, some sense of the limitation of small-signal anal-

output current.

7.2.2

Common-emitter power ampliï¬er stages are frequently used as output stage drivers of multi-

7.2.3
TRANSFORMER-COUPLED CLASS A POWER AMPLIFIER
If a load is connected directly to the transistor as in the common-emitter power ampliï¬er in
Figure 7.14, the quiescent current must pass through the load. î€€e load increases the required DC
power and reduces eï¬ƒciency since the quiescent current through the load does not contribute to
the AC signal component. To circumvent this problem, the load is commonly transformer coupled
to the transistor as shown in Figure 7.18.
î€€e transformer is used as an impedance matching element. To transfer a signiï¬cant
amount of power to a low impedance load, such as a the voice coil of a loudspeaker which is
typically between 4 and 15 , it is necessary to use an output matching transformer.

7.3
CLASS B POWER AMPLIFIERS
Class B ampliï¬ers are widely used because of the low standby DC current requirements, unlike
Class A ampliï¬ers that require an operating point characterized by a large collector current. î€€e
reduction in the standby or DC current requirement in Class B operation is achieved by biasing
each of the two transistors at cut-oï¬€. î€€is in turn reduces the quiescent collector power dissipation
in the transistors.
Since Class B power ampliï¬er operation has transistors conducting in only one half of the
period of the input signal, two transistors are necessary in a push-pull arrangement to add the two
halves of the cycle for the reconstruction of the entire ampliï¬ed sinusoid, shown in Figure 7.20.
î€€e transformer load coupling shown in the Figure 7.7 is widely used. î€€e collector supply is fed
into the center tap of the output transformer primary. î€€e base bias current is fed into the center
tap of the input transformer secondary.

characteristic of BJTs. î€€is leads to distortion near the zero crossings of the output current iO.

î€€e input characteristics and the resulting output in Figure 7.21 clearly shows the load current
distortion. î€€is distortion is referred to as the crossover distortion. Crossover distortion is best
understood by studying Figure 7.22. î€€e nonlinear input/output characteristic of the push-pull
conï¬guration is shown. î€€e exponential nature of the curve indicates high input resistance at low
signal levels. Little base current ï¬‚ows until the base-emitter voltage exceeds V. î€€e resulting col-
lector current will also be small until the input voltage is suï¬ƒciently high since the IC is essentially
proportional to the base current. î€€e resulting output from a push-pull Class B conï¬guration is
a sinusoid that exhibits crossover distortion.
It is customary to treat only one transistor when analyzing the push-pull conï¬guration since
each is operating at identical currents and voltages into an identical load, under the assumption
that the transistors have identical characteristics. î€€e output characteristic with load line of one
of the transistors in the Class B ampliï¬er of Figure 7.20 is shown in Figure 7.23.
î€€e DC load line per transistor is one-half of the resistance of the output transformer pri-
mary. If high-quality output transformers are used, the resistance of the primary can be assumed
to be negligible. î€€erefore, the DC load line has an inï¬nite negative slope. î€€e AC load line has

7.3.1
COMPLEMENTARY CLASS B (PUSH-PULL) OUTPUT STAGE
A simpliï¬ed version of a complementary Class B ampliï¬er arrangement that is often used as an
integrated circuit output stage is shown in Figure 7.24. î€€is arrangement uses complementary
transistors (one pnp and one npn) in common-collector conï¬guration. By using complementary
transistors, the need for input and output transformers is eliminated. î€€e transfer characteristic
is identical to that of the transformer coupled Class B power ampliï¬er.
î€€e principle of operation of the simpliï¬ed complementary Class B output stage is as fol-
lows. For jVij < VBE.on/, both Q1 and Q2 are cutoï¬€. When Vi > VBE.on/; Q1 turns on and enters
the forward saturation region. Q2 remains in cutoï¬€. î€€e upper limit on Vo occurs when Q1
saturates at Vi D VCC   VCE1.sat/. For negative values of Vi, the same situation holds true with
when the roles of Q1 and Q2 are reversed. î€€e lower limit on Vo occurs when Q2 saturates at
Vi D  .VCC   VCE2.sat//.
CMOS power buï¬€ers in the inverter conï¬guration, as discussed in Chapter 4 (Book 1), are
commonly used to interface CMOS logic to saturated logic (e.g., TTL). î€€is practice is common
in instances where a conventional CMOS gate may not be able to supply the required input power,
while at the same time provide suï¬ƒciently low output voltages for the TTL gates.

7.4
CLASS AB POWER AMPLIFIERS
In Section 7.3, Class B power ampliï¬ers were shown to have a maximum eï¬ƒciency of approxi-
mately 78.5%. Unfortunately, feedback was required to eliminate the nonlinear transfer charac-
teristic caused by crossover distortion.
An alternate method to feedback for reducing or nearly eliminating crossover distortion
is to bias each transistor in the push-pull conï¬guration so that they are barely in the forward
active region with Vi D 0. One possible conï¬guration to achieve this bias condition to eliminate
crossover distortion is shown in Figure 7.26. î€€is class of ampliï¬er is called a Class AB power
ampliï¬er/output stage since the transistors are always biased as in Class A operation, but is biased
at a fraction of the peak load current as in Class B operation.

It is interesting to compare the eï¬ƒciency of well-designed ampliï¬ers as a function of output
signal strength (Figure 7.29). î€€e output power of all ampliï¬ers is proportional to the square of

a parabolic eï¬ƒciency curve. Class B ampliï¬ers are characterized by DC power that is proportional
to signal strength: a linear eï¬ƒciency curve results. Class AB ampliï¬ers lie between the two other
classes with Class B as an upper limit.

7.5. DISTORTION
547
7.5
DISTORTION
When the output signal waveform of an ampliï¬er diï¬€ers in general shape from the input signal
waveform, the output is said to be distorted. In particular, if a single-frequency input to an am-
pliï¬er results in an output composed of the input frequency and other frequencies, the ampliï¬er
has distorted the signal. î€€e creation of additional frequencies is typically the result of non-linear
distortion.
Earlier, it was shown that large input signals to ampliï¬ers caused the ampliï¬er under test to
yield distorted output signals. In this section, a systematic description of distortion is developed.
With this description, two common industry deï¬nitions of distortion are developed and related
to each other.
An incrementally linear power ampliï¬erâ´ has a transfer characteristic described by
vO D VDC C a1vi;
(7.74)
where
vO  the output voltage,
vi  the input voltage (AC and DC components possible),
a1  the voltage gain,
VDC  the DC oï¬€set voltage at the output.
î€€e principle of superposition applies to incrementally linear systems in a unique manner. î€€at
is, if
vO1 D VDC C a1vi1
and
vO2 D VDC C a1vi2;
(7.75)
then the total output of a incrementally linear system is,
vO D VDC C a1.vi1 C vi2/:
(7.76)
In electronic ampliï¬ers, the DC component, VDC, consists of the bias or quiescent point of the
circuit.
Superposition no longer applies when the transfer characteristic is non-linear. Figure 7.31
shows a transfer characteristic of a power ampliï¬er.
î€€e region in Figure 7.31 that is not clipped is described by the power series,
vO D VDC C a1vi C a2v2

Equation (7.79) shows that harmonics are generated by the non-linear transfer characteristic.
Harmonics are sinusoidal terms that are multiples of the fundamental frequency, !, that is the
same frequency as the input signal. For linear power ampliï¬ers, the presence of harmonics is
undesirable and are the direct result of non-linear distortion.
Additionally, there is a shift in the DC or quiescent point by the addition of a term con-
taining the constant for the squared term in the power series. î€€e fundamental .cos !t/ term
also has increased beyond the ampliï¬cation constant associated with the fundamental by a term
proportional to the constant for the cubed term in the power series.
Equation (7.79) can be represented by a Fourier Series,
vO D VDC C A0 C A1 cos !t C A2 cos 2!t C A3 cos 3!t C    ;
(7.80)
where A0; A1; A2, and A3 are the Fourier coeï¬ƒcients. One method that may be used to determine
the Fourier coeï¬ƒcients is a method due to Espley.â¶ A pure sinusoidal signal voltage is applied to
the input of the power ampliï¬er. î€€e input and output waveforms are sampled at several times
intervals to obtain the Fourier coeï¬ƒcients of Equation (7.80). To evaluate the four coeï¬ƒcients
âµî€€is approximation is valid in typical ampliï¬ers where distortion is relatively small. In cases of extremely large distortion,
higher-order terms must be considered.

pp. 1439â€“1446, October, 1933.
