As an example, state-of-the-art audio power ampliï¬ers that incorporate negative feedback to
compensate for non-linearlity typically have THD of less than 0.003% at low frequencies and low

7.5. DISTORTION
551
output power levels.â· In cable television applications, the radio frequency (RF) power ampliï¬ers
also require very low distortion levels to deliver acceptable picture quality.
Because the distortion levels are so low, the method of Espley using the direct measure of
the transfer characteristic yields inaccurate distortion data due to noise and other spurious factors.
Instead, it is common practice to test ampliï¬er non-linearlity directly by using a pure sinusoidal
input. At the output, the harmonics are measured directly using a spectrum analyzer which dis-
plays the rms magnitude (or power) of the frequency components of the output signal. î€€e fun-
damental and harmonics appear on the CRT of the spectrum analyzer as spikes. Measurement
of the peak of the harmonic spikes relative to the fundamental spike yields the ampliï¬er THD.
Other analyzers (e.g., audio analyzers) perform the above operation automatically and display the
THD on its front panel.
Another method, commonly used in RF and microwave electronics, for ï¬nding the amount
of distortion of an ampliï¬er is the two-tone ratio (TTR) method for determining harmonic and
intermodulation distortion (IMD) products. In the TTR method, two signals with identical am-
plitudes that are separated by some frequency are combined and used as the input to the ampliï¬er.
î€€e output frequency components are then analyzed for distortion.

In the TTR method, new sum and diï¬€erence frequencies of the two input frequencies are created
and are called intermodulation (IMD) products or sometimes â€œbeatâ€ frequencies. î€€e new sum
and diï¬€erence frequencies are not harmonically related to either of the two fundamental input
frequencies.
î€€ere are several reasons for using the TTR method over single frequency harmonic mea-
surement methods. î€€ey include:
â€¢ Diï¬ƒculty in generating a pure sinusoid. All real signal generators have some harmonic
content.
â€¢ In single octaveâ¸ systems (those systems that only operate in one octave) with multiple
sinusoids closely spaced in frequency within the single octave, third order IMD products
are of interest.
î€€e relationship between the results of the TTR results and the harmonic coeï¬ƒcients for the

input signals are adjusted to equal the total input power of a single sinusoid for the harmonic
measurement. î€€is relationship implies that the rms power of the two combined sinusoids for the
TTR is equal to the rms value of the single input for the harmonic measurement,

In Figure 7.31, as the output voltage starts to clip, another form of distortion takes place.

7.6. THERMAL CONSIDERATIONS
555
7.6
THERMAL CONSIDERATIONS
î€€e removal of heat from the BJT collector-base junction or the FET channel warrants consider-
able attention in power transistors due to the high power delivered to the load. For power BJTs,â¹
metallic heat sinks are often necessary to remove the heat to the surrounding air. When possible,
the metallic portion of the transistor case should make contact with the heat sink. However, it is
common practice to use a mica washer as electrical insulation between the case and the heat sink.
î€€e washer is used to electrically isolate the heat sink from the case because in some power BJT
packages, the collector is directly attached to the case.
A speciï¬cation for the maximum allowable collector-junction temperature is found in the
manufacturerâ€™s transistor speciï¬cations. î€€is temperature is usually 150Ä±C for silicon devices. Ex-
ceeding this temperature may cause irreparable damage to the transistor. î€€e operating junction
temperature, Tj, is dependent on the ambient temperature, Ta (typically 25Ä±C), the thermal resis-
tance, T , of the heat transfer path from the junction to the surrounding and the power dissipated,
PD. î€€is relationship is expressed as:
Tj D Ta C T PD
in Ä±C:
(7.101)
î€€e total thermal resistance, T has the units of Celsius/Watt.
î€€e power dissipated by the transistor is almost entirely at the collector junction. î€€erefore,
the power dissipated is,

(7.102)

î€€e total thermal resistance is found by solving the heat transfer network analogous to an electrical
network shown in Figure 7.34.
î€€e current source represents the heat generated (or power dissipated by the BJT), and the
three resistances represent the thermal resistances of the junction to case (jc), case to heat sink
(cs), and heat sink to the ambient environment (sa ). î€€e total thermal resistance is,
T D jc C cs C sa D ja:
(7.104)
For power transistors, the values of jc is typically about 10Ä±C/W. If the transistor case and the
heat sink are insulated by a mica washer, cs has values of around 0:5Ä±C/W. î€€e primary mode of
heat removal from a power transistor is conduction by the heat sink and convection from the heat
sink to the ambient surroundings. By blowing air over the heat sink, more heat may be removed.
â¹î€€e discussion that follows is equally applicable to FETs.

Casual inspection of Equation (7.104) indicates that by removing the heat sink in the ther-

the case to ambient convection and radiation has signiï¬cantly higher thermal resistance thereby
reducing the ability of the device to dissipate heat: power handling capacity is actually diminished
for this case.
Not shown in Figure 7.34 are associated thermal capacitances in parallel with each resis-
tance. î€€e capacitance is used to model transient thermal behaviour. î€€e thermal time constant
(resistance times capacitance) is long for the external components of the thermal model, but short
for the transistor itself.
îermal runaway can result if the rate of increase in the power dissipation with junction
temperature exceeds the ability of the heat transfer network to remove the heat. î€€e dissipated
heat energy removed by the networks is found by diï¬€erentiating Equation (7.101) with respect

7.7
CONCLUDING REMARKS
Diï¬€erent classes of ampliï¬ers designed for the transfer of high power to loads were analyzed. î€€e
analysis of the ampliï¬ers required the use of large-signal analysis methods. Because of the large
signal excursions, the output waveforms experience distortion.
î€€e most commonly used power ampliï¬er/output stage classiï¬cations are the Class A, B,
and AB. In Class A operation, the transistor conducts over the whole period (360Ä±) of the input
signal. In Class B operation, each of the two transistors conducts over half the period (180Ä±). In
Class AB operation, each transistor conducts over a time greater than half the period.
Since high powers may be delivered by these circuits, the conversion of DC power to signal
power must be eï¬ƒcient. î€€e theoretical maximum eï¬ƒciencies for the diï¬€erent Classes of am-
pliï¬ers are: 25% for Class A, 50% for Class A output transformer coupled ampliï¬ers, 78.5% for
Class B, and less than 78.5% for Class AB operation.
Distortion analysis was presented with a comparison between the Total Harmonic Distor-
tion and Two-Tone Ratio methods of measurement.
Since power ampliï¬ers deliver high power to their loads, the transistor may dissipate large
amounts of power in the form of heat. î€€erefore, a heat transfer model was analyzed and related
to the stability factor.
Summary Design Example: Public Address (PA) System Ampliï¬er
In most audio electronic systems, the object is to drive one or more speakers. Typically, these
speakers can be modelled as 8  devices. î€€erefore, the output stage of audio electronic systems
require the ability to provide maximum power transfer to a very low resistance device. One com-
mon method to drive low resistance devices is through the use of an output transformer. î€€e
purpose of the output transformer is to provide for impedance matching between the active de-
vices and the speaker.
î€€e output stage of a PA system will require the application of an electronic signal to an
8  speaker. Although PA systems generally do not require high audio ï¬delity, a low distortion
system is desired since some music may be ampliï¬ed on the system. For a low to moderate power
PA system, the rated (or full) load power can be in the order of 25 W, with an overload capacity
of 10%. A single rail C24 V volt power supply is commonly used. î€€e output resistance of the
circuit that drives the output stage is 600 .
Design an output power ampliï¬er stage to fulï¬ll the requirements given.

C H A P T E R
8
Feedback Ampliï¬er Principles
Feedback is the process of combining a portion of the output of a system with the system input to
achieve modiï¬ed performance characteristics. Negative feedback is especially import in ampliï¬er
design as it produces several signiï¬cant beneï¬ts. î€€e primary beneï¬ts are:
â€¢ î€€e gain of the ampliï¬er is stabilized against variation in the characteristic parameters of
the active devices due to voltage or current supply changes, temperature changes, or de-
vice degradation with age. Similarly, ampliï¬er performance is stabilized within a group of
ampliï¬ers that have, by necessity, active devices with diï¬€erent characteristic parameters.
â€¢ î€€e input and output impedances of the ampliï¬er can be selectively increased or decreased.
â€¢ Non-linear signal distortion is reduced.
â€¢ î€€e midband frequency range is increased. Discussion of this aspect is delayed until Chap-
ter 11 (Book 3).
It is a rare occurrence when beneï¬ts come without a price. In the case of negative feedback, the
above listed beneï¬ts are accompanied by two primary drawbacks:
â€¢ î€€e gain of the circuit is reduced. In order to regain the losses due to feedback, additional
ampliï¬cation stages must be included in the system design. î€€is adds complexity, size,
weight, and cost to the ï¬nal design.
â€¢ î€€ere is a possibility for oscillation to occur. Oscillation will destroy the basic gain properties
of the ampliï¬er. Discussion of this aspect will also be delayed until Chapter 11 (Book 3).
In this chapter the beneï¬ts of negative feedback are considered. Basic deï¬nitions are followed
by a general discussion of the properties of a feedback system. Ampliï¬ers are divided into four
categories of feedback topology and the speciï¬c properties of each topological type are derived.
While the emphasis of discussions must focus on circuit analysis techniques, a clear understanding
of the of feedback in general, and eï¬€ects of circuit topology in particular, is a necessity for good
feedback ampliï¬er design.
î€€e Summary Design Example explores the common design practice of modifying existing
circuitry to meet new, but similar, performance speciï¬cations. Minimal alteration of the existing
design is explored as a possible, discretionary design criterion.

582
8. FEEDBACK AMPLIFIER PRINCIPLES
8.1
BASIC FEEDBACK CONCEPTS
î€€e basic topology of a feedback ampliï¬er is shown in Figure 8.1. î€€is ï¬gure shows a feedback
system in its most general form: each signal symbolized with fX. /g can take the form of either
a voltage or a current, and travels only in the direction of the indicated arrows. î€€e triangular
symbol is a linear ampliï¬er of gain, A, as has been described in several of the preceding Chapters.
î€€e rectangle indicates a feedback network that samples the output signal, scales it by a factor, f ,
and passes it forward to the input of the system. î€€e circular symbol is a summing (or mixing)
junction that subtracts the feedback signal, Xf , from the inputs. Subtraction of the two inputs at
the summing junction is a key factor in negative feedback systems.

Figure 8.1: Basic negative feedback topology.
î€€e system can be mathematically modeled in the following fashion. î€€e output of the
ampliï¬er, Xo, is related to its input signal XÄ± by a linear ampliï¬cation factor (gain), A, often
called the forward or open-loop gain:
Xo D A.XÄ±/:
(8.1)
Since the quantities Xo and XÄ± can be either voltage or current signals, the forward gain, A, can
be a voltage gain, a current gain, a transconductance, or a transresistance.Â¹ î€€e feedback signal,
Xf (a fraction of the output signal, Xo) is then subtracted
XÄ± D .Xi   Xf / D .Xi   fXo/;
(8.2)
where f is the feedback ratio deï¬ning the relationship between Xf and Xo:
Xf D fXo:
(8.3)
î€€e feedback ratio, f , can also be a ratio of voltages, currents, transconductance, or transresis-
tance. In order to have stable negative feedback, it is necessary that the mathematical sign of f be the
same as that of A.Â² î€€us, the product, Af , called the loop gain, is a positive, dimensionless quantity.
Â¹Gain quantities are ratios of an output quantity to an input quantity. Transresistance implies the ratio of an output voltage to
an input current: transconductance is the ratio of an output current to an input voltage.
Â²î€€is mathematical sign identity is necessary in the midband frequency region: Chapter 11 (Book 3) explores frequency-
dependent phase shifts in the gain and feedback quantities and their consequences.

î€€e input-output relationship for the overall system is derived from Equations (8.1) and (8.2):
Xo D

(8.4)
î€€e overall gain of the system including the eï¬€ects of feedback is then written as:
Af D Xo

(8.5)
Notice that Af does not need to be either a voltage or current gain: it will have the same di-
mensions as the forward gain, A. Equation (8.5) has special signiï¬cance in the study of feedback
systems and is called the basic feedback equation. î€€e denominator of the basic feedback equation
is identiï¬ed as the return diï¬€erence, D, also referred to as the amount of feedback:
D D 1 C Af:
(8.6)
î€€e return diï¬€erence, for negative feedback systems, has magnitude larger than unity (in the
midband frequency region) and is often speciï¬ed in decibels:
DdB D 20 log10 j1 C Af j:
(8.7)
î€€e return diï¬€erence quantiï¬es the degradation in gain due to the addition of feedback to the
system. It also plays a signiï¬cant role in quantifying changes in input and output impedance and
frequency bandwidth.
î€€e derivation of the basic feedback equation is based on two basic idealized assumptions:
â€¢ î€€e reverse transmission through the ampliï¬er is zero (applying a signal at the output pro-
duces no signal at the input).
â€¢ î€€e forward transmission (left to right in Figure 8.1) through the feedback network is zero.
While these assumptions are impossible to meet in practice, a reasonable approximation is ob-
tained with the following realistic requirements:
â€¢ î€€e reverse transmission through the ampliï¬er is negligible compared to the reverse trans-
mission through the feedback network.
â€¢ î€€e forward transmission through the feedback network is negligible compared to the for-
ward transmission through the ampliï¬er.
In most feedback ampliï¬ers the ampliï¬er is an active device with signiï¬cant forward gain and
near-zero reverse gain; the feedback network is almost always a passive network. î€€us, in the
forward direction, the large active gain will exceed the passive attenuation signiï¬cantly. Similarly,
in the reverse direction, the gain of the feedback network, albeit typically small, is signiï¬cantly

584
8. FEEDBACK AMPLIFIER PRINCIPLES
greater than the near-zero reverse gain of the ampliï¬er. In almost every electronic application, the
above stated requirements for the use of the basic feedback equation are easily met by the typical
feedback ampliï¬er.
Some of the drawbacks and beneï¬ts of feedback systems can be investigated on a simple
level by looking at the properties of the basic feedback equation (Equation (8.5)):Â³
1. îe gain of the circuit is reduced. It has been already shown that the overall gain is the gain
of the simple ampliï¬er without feedback divided the return diï¬€erence, which is larger in
magnitude than one.
2. îere is a possibility for oscillation to occur. It will be shown in Chapters 10 and 11 (Book 3)
that the gain decreases and the phase of the gain of an ampliï¬er changes as frequency in-
creases. î€€is change in phase may cause the loop gain, Af , to change sign from positive to
negative. If this change in sign occurs at the same frequency that the magnitude of the loop
gain approaches unity, the return diï¬€erence approaches zero at that frequency. Division by
zero indicates an instability: that instability is realized as an oscillation. Linear oscillators
are discussed in Chapter 12 (Book 4).
3. îe gain of the ampliï¬er is stabilized against variation in the characteristic parameters of the
active devices. It has been shown in previous chapters, that the gain, A, of an ampliï¬er
is highly dependent on the parameters of the active devices. î€€ese parameters are highly
dependent on temperature, bias conditions, and manufacturing tolerances. It is therefore
desirable to design ampliï¬ers that are reasonably insensitive to the variation of the device
parameters.
î€€e relationship between the diï¬€erential change in gain due to device parameter variation
with and without feedback is obtained by diï¬€erentiating Equation (8.5):
d Af D

(8.8)
which is more typically expressed as:

.1 C Af / > 1I

or current) of the input and output quantities. Discussion of these properties of feedback systems is found in Section 8.3.

Example 8.1

5. îe midband frequency range is increased. While this property is discussed extensively in
Chapter 11 (Book 3), it can be considered a special case of the reduction in gain variation.
As frequencies increase, the performance parameters of an ampliï¬er degrade. Similarly,
coupling and bypass capacitors will degrade low-frequency performance. Feedback reduces
the eï¬€ects of these frequency-dependent degradations and thereby increases the frequency
band over which the ampliï¬er has stable gain.

588
8. FEEDBACK AMPLIFIER PRINCIPLES
8.2
FEEDBACK AMPLIFIER TOPOLOGIES
As has been seen in the previous section, general discussions provide great insight into many of the
properties of feedback systems. In order to consider the design of electronic feedback ampliï¬ers,
it is necessary, however, to specify the details of the feedback sampling and mixing processes
and the circuits necessary to accomplish these operations. î€€e sampling and mixing processes
have a profound eï¬€ect on the input impedance, the output impedance, and the deï¬nition of the
forward gain quantity that undergoes quantiï¬ed change due to the application of feedback.â´ î€€is
section will analyze the various idealized feedback conï¬gurations: Section 8.3 will look at practical
feedback conï¬gurations.
As has been previously stated, both the mixing and the sampling process for a feedback
ampliï¬er can utilize either voltages or currents. Voltage mixing (subtraction) implies a series con-
nection of voltages at the input of the ampliï¬er: current mixing implies a shunt connection. Volt-
age sampling implies a shunt connection of the sampling probes across the output voltage: current
sampling implies a series connection so that the output current ï¬‚ows into the sampling network.
Either type of mixing can be combined with either type of sampling. î€€us, a feedback ampli-
ï¬er may have one of four possible combinations of the mixing and sampling processes. î€€ese
four combinations are commonly identiï¬ed by a hyphenated term: (mixing topology)-(sampling
topology). î€€e four types are:
â€¢ shunt-shunt feedback (current mixing & voltage sampling)
â€¢ shunt-series feedback (current mixing & current sampling)
â€¢ series-shunt feedback (voltage mixing & voltage sampling)
â€¢ series-series feedback (voltage mixing & current sampling)
î€€e four basic feedback ampliï¬er topologies are shown schematically in Figure 8.3. A source
and a load resistance have been attached to model complete operation. In each diagram the input,
feedback and output quantities are shown properly as voltages or currents. Forward gain, A, must
be deï¬ned as the ratio of the output sampled quantity divided by the input quantity that under-
goes mixing. As such it is a transresistance, current gain, voltage gain, or transconductance. î€€e
feedback network, as described by the feedback ratio .f /, must sample the output quantity and
present a quantity to the mixer that is of the same type (current or voltage) as the input quantity.
As such it is a transconductance, current gain, voltage gain, or transresistance. Table 8.1 is listing
of the appropriate quantities mixed at the input, the output sampled quantity, the forward gain,
and the feedback ratio for each of the four feedback ampliï¬er topologies. It is important to re-
member that the product, Af , must be dimensionless and, in the midband region of operation,
positive.
â´For any gain topology, all forward gain quantities may undergo change: the gain quantity listed in Table 8.1 is the single
quantity that is altered as described by the basic feedback equation (Equation (8.5)).

8.3
PRACTICAL FEEDBACK CONFIGURATIONS
Previous discussions of feedback and feedback conï¬gurations have been limited to idealized sys-
tems and ampliï¬ers. î€€e four idealized feedback schematic diagrams of Figure 8.3 identify the
forward-gain ampliï¬er and the feedback network as two-port networks with a very speciï¬c prop-
erty: each is a device with one-way gain. Realistic electronic feedback ampliï¬ers can only approx-
imate that idealized behavior. In addition, in practical feedback ampliï¬ers there is always some
interaction between the forward-gain ampliï¬er and the feedback network. î€€is interaction most
often takes the form of input and output resistive loading of the forward-gain ampliï¬er. î€€e di-
vision of the practical feedback ampliï¬er into its forward-gain ampliï¬er and feedback network
is also not always obvious. î€€ese apparent obstacles to using idealized feedback analysis can be
resolved through the use of two-port network relationships in the derivation of practical feed-
back ampliï¬er properties. Once ampliï¬er gain and impedance relationships have been derived,
the utility of the two-port representations becomes minimal and is typically discarded.
Feedback topology is determined through careful observation of the interconnection of the
feedback network and forward-gain ampliï¬er. Shunt mixing occurs at the input terminal of the
ampliï¬er. î€€us, shunt mixing is identiï¬ed by a connection of feedback network and the forward-
gain ampliï¬er at the input terminal of ï¬rst active device within the ampliï¬er, that is:
âµSeries mixing feedback ampliï¬ers that have resistors shunting the input require special care in the application of these formulas.
Also, feedback ampliï¬ers with series sampling and loads that shunt the output require similar care. See Section 8.3 for examples
of proper calculations.

8.3. PRACTICAL FEEDBACK CONFIGURATIONS
595
â€¢ at the base of a BJT for a common-emitter or common-collector ï¬rst stage,
â€¢ at the emitter of a BJT for a common-base ï¬rst stage,
â€¢ at the gate of a FET for a common-source or common-drain ï¬rst stage, or
â€¢ at the source of a FET for a common-gate ï¬rst stage.
Series mixing occurs in a loop that contains the input terminal of the forward-gain ampliï¬er
and the controlling port of the ï¬rst active device. î€€e controlling port of a BJT in the forward-
active region is the base-emitter junction: a FET in the saturation region is controlled by the
voltage across the gate-source input port. Series mixing is characterized by a circuit element which
is both connected to the output and in series with the input voltage and the input port of the ï¬rst
active device.
Identiï¬cation of the sampling is derived from direct observation of the connection of the
output of the basic forward ampliï¬er and the feedback network. Shunt sampling is typically char-
acterized by a direct connection of the feedback network to the output node: series sampling im-
plies a series connection of the ampliï¬er output, the feedback network, and the load. Two tests
performed at the feedback ampliï¬er output can aid in the determination of sampling topology:
â€¢ If the feedback quantity vanishes for a short-circuit load, the output voltage must be the
sampled quantity. î€€us, zero feedback for a short-circuit load implies shunt sampling.
â€¢ If the feedback quantity vanishes for an open-circuit load, the output current must be the
sampled quantity. î€€us, zero feedback for a open-circuit load implies series sampling.
After the topological type has been identiï¬ed, each ampliï¬er must be transformed into a
form that allows for the use of the idealized feedback formulations. î€€is transformation includes
modeling the ampliï¬er and the feedback network with a particular two-port representation that
facilitates combination of elements. Once the transformations are accomplished, the ampliï¬er
performance parameters are easily obtained using the methods previously outlined in this Chap-
ter. î€€e particular operations necessary to transform each of the four feedback ampliï¬er topo-
logical types require separate discussion. î€€e examples in this section demonstrate the typical
interconnection of a basic forward ampliï¬er and a feedback network for each feedback topol-
ogy. While BJT ampliï¬ers form the examples of this section, the general approach applies to all
feedback ampliï¬ers regardless of active device type.
8.3.1

combined. A resistive load has been applied to the output port; and, since shunt-shunt feedback

î€€e application of the basic feedback equation to this circuit in its current form is not immediately
clear. It is necessary to transform the feedback ampliï¬er circuit into a form that allows for easy
application of the basic feedback equation, Equation (8.35). Such a transformation must meet
the previously stated feedback requirements:
â€¢ î€€e forward-gain ampliï¬er is to be a forward transmission system onlyâ€”its reverse trans-
mission must be negligible.
â€¢ î€€e feedback network is to be a reverse transmission system that presents a feedback current,
dependent on the output voltage, to the ampliï¬er input port.
While a mathematically rigorous derivation of the transformation is possible, greater insight to
the process comes with a heuristic approach.
î€€e two-port y-parameter representation, in conjunction with the shunt-shunt connection,
is used to describe the two main elements of this feedback ampliï¬er so that all the input port
elements of both two-port networks are in parallel. Similarly, all output port elements are in
parallel. It is well known that circuit elements in parallel may be rearranged and, so long as they
remain in parallel, the circuit continues to function in an identical fashion. Hence, it is possible,
for analysis purposes only, to conceptually move elements from one section of the circuit into another

â€¢ All reverse transadmittances, y12, (represented by current sources dependent on the output
voltage, vo) are placed in the modiï¬ed feedback circuit.

It is helpful to reiterate the procedure for ï¬nding the performance parameters of a feedback
ampliï¬er. In order to ï¬nd the feedback ampliï¬er performance parameters, the following procedure
is followed:
â€¢ Perform a DC analysis to obtain active device performance parameters
â€¢ Draw an AC equivalent circuit of the entire circuit
â€¢ Partition the circuit into its functional modules
â€¢ Load the Basic Forward Ampliï¬er
â€¢ Determine the Performance of the Loaded Forward Ampliï¬er.
â€¢ Apply the Feedback Relationships to Obtain Total Circuit Performance
â€¢ If necessary, transform these performance parameters into equivalent performance param-
eters as speciï¬ed.
î€€is procedure can be followed with all four feedback topologies in the same manner as
has been shown in Example 8.3. î€€e speciï¬cs of loading the basic forward ampliï¬er and the
application of the feedback formulas will vary according to the topology.

Once again, it is necessary to transform the feedback ampliï¬er circuit into a form that allows
for easy application of the basic feedback equation, Equation (8.43). Such a transformation must
meet the previously stated feedback requirements:
â€¢ î€€e forward-gain ampliï¬er is to be a forward transmission system onlyâ€”its reverse trans-
mission must be negligible.
â€¢ î€€e feedback network is to be a reverse transmission system that presents a feedback current,
dependent on the output voltage, to the ampliï¬er input port.
î€€e two-port g-parameter representation, in conjunction with the shunt-series connection,
is used to describe the two main elements of this feedback ampliï¬er so that all the input port
elements of both two-port networks are in parallel. In contrast, all output port elements are in
series. For analysis purposes only, elements are conceptually moved elements from one section of
the circuit into another (from the feedback circuit to the ampliï¬er circuit or the reverse). î€€e
necessary conceptual changes made for the transformation are:
â€¢ î€€e source resistance; all input admittances .g11/, and output impedances .g22/, are placed
in the modiï¬ed ampliï¬er circuit. î€€e load resistance is kept separate.
â€¢ All forward voltage gains .g21/ (represented by voltage sources dependent on the input
voltage, v1) are placed in the modiï¬ed ampliï¬er circuit, and

no eï¬€ect on the feedback, while opening the collector of the output BJT eliminates all feedback:
the sampling topology must be series sampling.

8.4
CONCLUDING REMARKS
î€€e fundamental advantages of the use of feedback in electronic ampliï¬ers have been described in
this chapter. Reduction in non-linear distortion, variation in ampliï¬er performance parameters,
and control of input and out impedance are all signiï¬cant beneï¬ts available through the use of
feedback.
î€€e analysis of feedback ampliï¬ers can be a complex process complicated by the interaction
of the basic forward ampliï¬er and the feedback network. It is, however, possible to simplify this
analysis process through the use of a few basic feedback relationships and a systematic analysis
method. î€€is analysis method consists of a seven step process:
â€¢ Identify the Feedback Topology
â€¢ Perform a DC Analysis to Obtain Active Device Performance Parameters
â€¢ Partition the AC Equivalent Circuit into its Functional Modules
â€¢ Load the Basic Forward Ampliï¬er
â€¢ Determine the Performance of the Loaded Forward Ampliï¬er
â€¢ Apply the Feedback Relationships to Obtain Total Circuit Performance
â€¢ If Necessary, Transform these Performance Parameters into Equivalent Performance Pa-
rameters as Speciï¬ed.
Vital to the analysis method are the partitioning of the circuit into its functional modules
and loading the basic forward ampliï¬er. Table 8.3 serves as an aid in that process, itemizing the
process variation due to feedback topology.
Another signiï¬cant beneï¬t of feedback, the increase in the midband frequency range, will
be extensively discussed in Chapter 11 (Book 3).
Summary Design Example
î€€e design process often involves the modiï¬cation of an existing design to meet new, but similar,
performance speciï¬cations. As an example, the feedback ampliï¬er shown below constructed with
enhancement-mode FETs with the following properties:
K D 1 mA=V
VT D 1:5 V
VA D 160 V:

It is desired to design an ampliï¬er with similar input and output resistance and a midband voltage
gain of 15. Redesign the ampliï¬er to meet the new design goals.
Solution:
If at all possible, a redesign process should minimize the number of changes. For the given
ampliï¬er, the FET AC parameters will remain the same if the quiescent drain currents are not
changed. î€€us, it seems advantageous to retain the same Q-poing. î€€is proposed redesign con-
straint limits changes to the feedback resistor .10 k/ and the load resistor .4:7 k/. While vari-
ation in either will alter the maximum symmetrical output swing, voltage gain is more strongly
dependent on the feedback resistor: less variation in quiescent output voltage will result in chang-
ing the feedback resistor. It is therefore decided to alter only the feedback resistor to accomplish the
new design goals.
î€€is ampliï¬er is a series-shunt feedback ampliï¬er: the gain parameter that is stabilized is the
voltage gain from the input port to the output port. In order to meet the new design goals the gain
with feedback must be (the input resistance of this ampliï¬er simply is the parallel combination of
the bias resistors and does not change with variation in feedback):

PROBLEMS
8.1.
î€€e voltage gain of an ampliï¬er is subject to a variation given by
A D 238 Ë™ 4 V=V:

ampliï¬er. î€€e feedback ampliï¬er must have no more than 0.15% variation in its voltage
gain.
(a) Determine the necessary feedback ratio to achieve this variation while achieving
maximum gain.
(b) What is the gain of the feedback ampliï¬er?
8.2.
An ampliï¬er with an open-loop voltage gain AV D 1000 Ë™ 100 is given. î€€e ampliï¬er
must be altered so that the voltage gain varies by no more than 0.5%.
(a) Find the feedback factor, f , to achieve this variation while achieving maximum
gain.
(b) Find the gain of the feedback ampliï¬er.
8.3.

a feedback network with an feedback factor f D 0:02. Determine the range of values of
the gain, AV , for which the closed-loop gain, AVf, is:
(a) 120 Ë™ 1
(b) 120 Ë™ 0:2

available are ampliï¬er stages having a gain of 2000 with 25% variation. Other constraints
require that feedback be applied only to each individual stage and that such stages be con-
nected in cascade.

Authorsâ€™ Biographies
î€€omas F. Schubert, Jr., and Ernest M. Kim are colleagues in the Electrical Engineering
Department of the Shiley-Marcos School of Engineering at the University of San Diego.
THOMAS F. SCHUBERT, JR.
î€€omas Schubert received BS, MS, and PhD degrees in Elec-
trical Engineering from the University of California at Irvine
(UCI). He was a member of the ï¬rst engineering graduating
class and the ï¬rst triple-degree recipient in engineering at UCI.
His doctoral work discussed the propagation of polarized light
in anisotropic media.
Dr. Schubert arrived at the University of San Diego in
August, 1987 as one of the two founding faculty of its new En-
gineering Program. From 1997â€“2003, he led the Department as
Chairman, a position that became Director of Engineering Pro-
grams during his leadership tenure. Prior to coming to USD, he
was at the Space and Communications Division of Hughes Air-
craft Company, the University of Portland, and Portland State
University. He is a Registered Professional Engineer in the State of Oregon.
In 2012, Dr. Schubert was awarded the Robert G. Quinn Award by the American So-
ciety of Engineering Education â€œin recognition of outstanding contributions in providing and
promoting excellence in engineering experimentation and laboratory instruction.â€

ERNEST M. KIM
Ernest Kim received his B.S.E.E. from the University of
